 Abstract 
 We present a digital quantum computation of two-hadron scattering in a Z 2 subscript 𝑍 2 Z_{2} italic_Z start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT lattice gauge theory in 1+1 dimensions. We prepare well-separated single-particle wave packets with desired momentum-space wavefunctions, and simulate their collision through digitized time evolution. Multiple hadronic wave packets can be produced using the efficient, systematically improvable algorithm of this work, achieving high fidelity with the target initial state.
Specifically, employing a trapped-ion quantum computer ( IonQ Forte ), we prepare up to three meson wave packets using 11 and 27 system qubits, and simulate collision dynamics of two meson wave packets for the smaller system.
Results for local observables are consistent with numerical simulations at early times, but decoherence effects limit evolution into long times. We demonstrate the critical role of high-fidelity initial states for precision measurements of state-sensitive observables, such as S 𝑆 S italic_S -matrix elements.
Our work establishes the potential of quantum computers in simulating hadron-scattering processes in strongly interacting gauge theories. 

 † † preprint: UMD-PP-025-02, IQuS@UW-21-102

 I Introduction

 A plethora of scattering experiments continue to serve as an excellent testing ground for verifying theoretical predictions for the structure of hadrons and nuclei  Accardi  et al. ( 2016 ); Anderle  et al. ( 2021 ); Deshpande  et al. ( 2005 ); Achenbach  et al. ( 2024 ) , searching for new particles and interactions beyond the Standard Model  Narain  et al. ( 2022 ); Bruning  et al. ( 2012 ); Shiltsev and Zimmermann ( 2021 ); Workman  et al. ( 2022 ); Kadan ( 2024 ); Abi  et al. ( 2021 ); Andringa  et al. ( 2016 ); Takeuchi ( 2023 ) , and investigating phases of matter in and out of equilibrium  Csernai ( 1994 ); Ludlam and Aronson ( 2005 ); Andronic  et al. ( 2018 ); Berges  et al. ( 2021 ); Lovato  et al. ( 2022 ) .
Many of these experiments involve atomic nuclei, which are composed of nucleons that interact via the strong force. Quantum chromodynamics (QCD) is the quantum theory of the strong force, which is a non-Abelian gauge theory that describes the interactions between the constituents of nucleons, quarks and gluons  Gross  et al. ( 2023 ) . QCD becomes strongly interacting at low energies, hence non-perturbative physics takes effect. Furthermore, the theory exhibits confinement at low energies: the interacting degrees of freedom are bound states of quarks and gluons. Classical computers  Joó  et al. ( 2019 ) , in conjunction with the lattice-QCD method  Wilson ( 1974 ) , have enabled first-principles non-perturbative studies of QCD observables  Aoki  et al. ( 2024 ); Davoudi  et al. ( 2022 ); Kronfeld  et al. ( 2022 ) .
In the lattice-QCD approach, the spacetime is discretized and time is rendered imaginary. Quantities are evaluated in a path-integral formalism using Monte Carlo methods, where in most scenarios, the action leads to a well-defined probability distribution  Montvay and Munster ( 1997 ); Rothe ( 2012 ); Gattringer and Lang ( 2010 ) .
Lattice QCD is most suitable for calculating static observables since it uses an Euclidean-time action. Nonetheless, it can also be employed to extract dynamical observables like scattering and decay amplitudes  Briceño  et al. ( 2015 ); Briceno  et al. ( 2018 ); Hansen and Sharpe ( 2019 ); Davoudi  et al. ( 2021a ) , thanks to theoretical developments that utilize the finite-volume nature of lattice-QCD calculations  Luscher ( 1986 , 1991 ) .
However, the application of such methods is limited to low-energy and low-inelasticity processes. Currently, first-principles predictions for general real-time scattering processes, e.g., those relevant for descriptions of high-energy particle collisions and the early universe, remain beyond the reach of the state-of-the-art lattice-QCD calculations  Gattringer and Langfeld ( 2016 ); Nagata ( 2022 ) . 

 An alternative to the Euclidean-time Lagrangian formulation of lattice gauge theories (LGTs) is the Hamiltonian formulation  Kogut and Susskind ( 1975 ) .
In this formulation, one constructs the Hamiltonian, and the associated Hilbert space, of a gauge theory on a discretized space but with a continuous time. Real-time processes are then computed from time evolution of states. Interest in the Hamiltonian LGTs has revived in the recent years thanks to novel computational paradigms that offer polynomial, instead of exponential, scaling of the computational resources with respect to system’s volume.
One paradigm is the tensor-network methods that achieve polynomial scaling by systematically controlling the entanglement generation in the state when possible, using suitable tensor-network ansatzes  Bañuls and Cichy ( 2020 ); Meurice  et al. ( 2022 ); Bañuls ( 2023 ); Cirac  et al. ( 2021 ) . These calculations are gaining attention for studying real-time dynamics in LGTs  Magnifico  et al. ( 2021 ); Silvi  et al. ( 2014 ); Tagliacozzo  et al. ( 2014 ); Rico  et al. ( 2014 ); Buyens  et al. ( 2016 ); Pichler  et al. ( 2016 ); Buyens  et al. ( 2017 ); Kühn  et al. ( 2015 ); Mathew  et al. ( 2025 ); Bañuls  et al. ( 2025 ) , including scattering problems  Rigobello  et al. ( 2021 ); Belyansky  et al. ( 2024 ); Papaefstathiou  et al. ( 2024 ); Jha  et al. ( 2024 ) .
Nonetheless, utilizing this tool for simulating high-dimensional models and non-Abelian gauge theories remains limited  Magnifico  et al. ( 2024 ); Kelman  et al. ( 2024 ); Yosprakob ( 2024 ) . The second novel computational paradigm uses quantum computers, leveraging the principles of quantum superposition and entanglement  Feynman ( 1982 ); Simon ( 2006 ); Lloyd ( 1996 ); Preskill ( 2021 ) , and offering exponential scaling in its (more conventional) building blocks of qubits  Nielsen and Chuang ( 2012 ) or its rapidly emerging alternatives of qudits  Chi  et al. ( 2023 ); Nikolaeva  et al. ( 2024 ) , fermions  Bravyi and Kitaev ( 2002 ) , or bosons  Chabaud and Walschaers ( 2023 ) .
LGTs subsequently can be simulated by mapping their Hilbert space to that of the constituent degrees of freedom of a quantum computer  Dalmonte and Montangero ( 2016 ); Bañuls  et al. ( 2020 ); Klco  et al. ( 2022 ); Bauer  et al. ( 2023a , b ); Halimeh  et al. ( 2023 ); Di Meglio  et al. ( 2024 ); Beck  et al. ( 2023 ) . One way to simulate a Hamiltonian time evolution is by expressing the time-evolution operator in terms of a universal set of single-qubit gates, and two-qubit entangling gates.
This approach, which is known as digital quantum simulation, is quantum-hardware agnostic, and will be the basis of this work. 

 In a pioneering work by Jordan, Lee, and Preskill  Jordan  et al. ( 2012 , 2014 ) , an algorithm for digital quantum simulation of scattering was proposed for a scalar field theory with quartic interactions. This algorithm adopts the S 𝑆 S italic_S -matrix approach in the scattering theory, where the “in” (“out”) state is taken to be single-particle wave packets long in the past (future) and far separated in space. The initial scattering state of well-separated single-particle wave packets is prepared from the adiabatic evolution of a known state. The known state is taken to be two well-separated single-particle wave packets in the non-interacting theory, and adiabatic evolution amounts to gradually turning on the interaction strength. Once the wave packets in the interacting theory are prepared, the state is evolved under the full Hamiltonian until they collide. Finally, measurements are performed by simulating detectors in the late-time regions.
Adiabatic state preparation has, nonetheless, several practical and theoretical drawbacks.
For example, it requires an overhead to compensate for wave-packet broadening during the evolution  Jordan  et al. ( 2012 ) .
Furthermore, adiabatic state preparation becomes computationally infeasible when a phase transition occurs on the evolution path  Farhi  et al. ( 2000 ); Surace  et al. ( 2024 ) —a situation that incurs, e.g., when one tries to prepare the ground state of the confined phase of a model from that of the deconfined phase.
In the context of quantum field theories, improvements to the adiabatic state preparation have been proposed by using more efficient bases  Barata  et al. ( 2021 ) , by traversing efficient paths within the adiabatic trajectory  Cohen and Oh ( 2024 ) , or by combining variational  Klco  et al. ( 2018 ); Atas  et al. ( 2021 ); Fromm  et al. ( 2024 ); Crippa  et al. ( 2024 ) and adiabatic  Chakraborty  et al. ( 2022 ); D’Anna  et al. ( 2024 ) methods in an scalable manner  Farrell  et al. ( 2024a , b ); Li  et al. ( 2024a ) .
Recently, algorithms for wave-packet preparation and two-particle scattering in (1+1)D scalar field theory have been demonstrated on quantum computers  Ingoldby  et al. ( 2025 ); Zemlevskiy ( 2024 ) , albeit limited to highly truncated fields and short-time evolutions, due to decoherence. Finally, a first attempt at observing inelastic scattering in a (1+1)D Ising field theory was reported in Ref.  Farrell  et al. ( 2025a ) . 

 Proposals exist for preparing the initial interacting wave packets by circumventing the adiabatic state preparation  Liu  et al. ( 2022 ); Turco  et al. ( 2024 , 2025 ); Kreshchuk  et al. ( 2023 ); Hite ( 2025 ) , including extraction of the scattering observables from appropriate asymptotic matrix elements directly  Li  et al. ( 2024b ); Briceño  et al. ( 2023 , 2021 ); Gustafson  et al. ( 2021 ); Peruzzo  et al. ( 2014 ); McClean  et al. ( 2016 ); Tilly  et al. ( 2022 ); Sharma  et al. ( 2024 ); Ciavarella ( 2020 ) .
Nonetheless, these approaches are either limited to massive excitations in the process or are challenging to apply to studies of high-energy particle collisions, including subsequent hadronization, fragmentation, and thermalization processes.
Another method that requires no adiabatic interpolation and, instead, creates the wave packets directly in the interacting theory is that proposed in Ref.  Rigobello  et al. ( 2021 ) in a (1+1)D U(1) LGT using tensor-network methods, and used in Ref.  Chai  et al. ( 2023 ) to study fermion-antifermion scattering.
We recently provided an improvement to this approach in Ref.  Davoudi  et al. ( 2024a ) by using an efficient hybrid classical-quantum variational algorithm that creates wave packets of bound excitations in confining gauge theories in (1+1)D. We further demonstrated the algorithm’s viability in the noisy intermediate-scale quantum (NISQ) era by implementing it on a trapped-ion quantum computer. 

 In the present work, we further optimize our state-preparation method by parameterizing a position-space rather than a momentum-space form, which proves to be more efficient. We employ this algorithm together with a time-evolution algorithm to simulate scattering in a (1+1)D Z 2 subscript 𝑍 2 Z_{2} italic_Z start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT LGT with dynamical fermions.
This LGT exhibits non-perturbative features, such as confinement. It, thus, serves as a testing ground for simulating the complex theory of QCD, and at the same time, it may provide insights into the physics of confinement  Magnifico  et al. ( 2019 ); Charles  et al. ( 2024 ); Mildenberger  et al. ( 2022 ); Irmejs  et al. ( 2023 ); Frank  et al. ( 2020 ); Borla  et al. ( 2020 , 2022 ); Das  et al. ( 2023 ); Samajdar  et al. ( 2023 ); König  et al. ( 2020 ); Yarloo  et al. ( 2019 ) . First, we show a resource-efficient method for preparing the initial state of well-separated single-particle wave packets with tunable parameters, like their spatial width and central momentum.
The new approach of this work allows for various controlled approximations to the target state, and in the worst case, relies on an ansatz that uses only a polynomial number of parameters in the system size. Moreover, for theories with finite correlation length, the algorithm’s cost scales polynomially with the correlation length rather than with the system size. We demonstrate our state-preparation algorithm by preparing multiple single-particle wave packets on an IonQ Forte quantum computer  Chen  et al. ( 2024 ) for two different lattice sizes, and compare it against the very high fidelity (and thus quantum-resource intensive) state obtained using the noiseless emulator, observing good agreement. 

 Importantly in this work, we proceed with evolving the two wave packets till they collide.
Various methods have been proposed for simulating time evolution in the recent years  Low and Chuang ( 2019 ); Kirby  et al. ( 2021 ); Chen  et al. ( 2021 ); Kalev and Hen ( 2021 ); Low and Wiebe ( 2018 ) . Trotter product expansion of the unitary time-evolution operator, nonetheless, was found to be the most resource-efficient algorithm for our NISQ-era application.
Our goal is to investigate the type of scattering observables accessible using near-term NISQ-era devices.
We first quantum compute on the same hardware the real-time evolution of local observables.
We show that these observables agree reasonably well with the ideal result at early times, and employing a symmetry-based noise mitigation during post-processing has little effect.
We further show, with the aid of the noisy emulator provided by IonQ , that additional noise-mitigation circuits could be implemented to retrieve the signal for observables that are more deviated from their idea values.
Finally, we highlight the importance of preparing high-fidelity initial states by comparing the probability of returning to the initial state for different degrees of initial-state infidelities.
This quantity is ultimately related to the scattering S 𝑆 S italic_S -matrix, and for unstable single-particle states, it plays a role in calculating the decay width  Yamada  et al. ( 2023 ); Izrailev and Castañeda-Mendoza ( 2006 ); Ciavarella ( 2020 ) .
We find that such a non-local observable, as expected, is highly sensitive to the approximations made in the initial state. 

 The paper is organized as follows.
In Sec.  II , we provide the necessary theoretical background for constructing the single-particle wave packets and the ansatz used to create them.
Section  III describes the different components of the quantum algorithm employed to prepare and evolve the wave packets.
Section  IV presents both the quantum hardware and emulator results, along with the noise-mitigation methods used.
Finally, we conclude, and provide an outlook, in Sec.  V . Several appendices supplement our results and analyses. 

 II Theoretical formalism

 This section contains the required theoretical background for implementing the scattering protocol of this work on a quantum hardware. First, an efficient formulation of a Z 2 subscript 𝑍 2 Z_{2} italic_Z start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT LGT in (1+1)D is derived in Sec.  II.1 , then an ansatz for building the lowest-energy particle excitation in each momentum sector is developed and optimized in Sec.  II.2 . Finally, numerical evidence for the performance of the optimized ansatz, and its accuracy in regimes of arbitrary coupling and mass, are presented in Sec.  II.3 . 

 II.1 Z 2 subscript 𝑍 2 Z_{2} italic_Z start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT LGT in (1+1)D with dynamical matter

 Consider a Z 2 subscript 𝑍 2 Z_{2} italic_Z start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT LGT coupled to a single flavor of fermions on a spatial lattice with a periodic boundary condition (PBC).

Matter contents in this theory are staggered fermions  Kogut and Susskind ( 1975 ); Banks  et al. ( 1976 ) : fermions and antifermions live on the even and odd sites of the lattice, respectively, implying that N 𝑁 N italic_N must be an even number, and the staggered lattice corresponds to a physical system with N P ⁢ \coloneq ⁢ N / 2 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 \coloneq 𝑁 2 N_{P}\coloneq N/2 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_N / 2 lattice sites.

 The gauge degrees of freedom in (1+1)D LGTs are not dynamical in nature, and are often rotated away when open boundary condition (OBC) are in place. In this case, only the fermionic-matter degrees of freedom survive but at the expense of yielding a non-local Hamiltonian.
Such an elimination of the gauge bosons is achieved by transforming the fermionic fields as

(Either of these options yields the same spectrum so we choose a positive sign.) In other words, we use the convention that the action of the transformed gauge-link operator Σ ~ 𝐱 superscript ~ Σ 𝐱 \widetilde{\Sigma}^{\mathbf{x}} over~ start_ARG roman_Σ end_ARG start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT bold_x end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT is now restricted to the link connecting lattice sites N − 1 𝑁 1 N-1 italic_N - 1 and 0 0 . 1 1 1 While the Hamiltonian in Eq. ( 9 ) appears to distinguish the last link from the rest of the links, lattice translational symmetry of the original formulation is still preserved in this merely gauge-transformed formulation. 

 One can then reduce the bosonic Hilbert space to the local Hilbert space at the last link, and a generic state in this Hilbert space is now given by

 (13) 

 The ψ n subscript 𝜓 𝑛 \psi_{n} italic_ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT and ψ n † subscript superscript 𝜓 † 𝑛 \psi^{\dagger}_{n} italic_ψ start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT † end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT field operators act on the fermionic Hilbert space in the same manner as ξ n subscript 𝜉 𝑛 \xi_{n} italic_ξ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT and ξ n † subscript superscript 𝜉 † 𝑛 \xi^{\dagger}_{n} italic_ξ start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT † end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT operators act on the state in Eq. ( 1 ).
The action of the gauge-link and the electric-field operator at site n 𝑛 n italic_n is restricted to the reduced bosonic Hilbert space. The electric Hamiltonian, nonetheless, has become non-local, where the non-locality originates from the dependence of Σ ~ n 𝐳 subscript superscript ~ Σ 𝐳 𝑛 \widetilde{\Sigma}^{\mathbf{z}}_{n} over~ start_ARG roman_Σ end_ARG start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT bold_z end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT on the fermion-number occupations up to site n 𝑛 n italic_n .
Non-locality is the cost to pay to render all states physical in this Hilbert space (there is no local Gauss’s law constraint anymore in the new formulation).

 We refer to the Hamiltonian in Eq. ( 5 ) and the Hilbert space spanned by states in Eq. ( 1 ) as the explicit gauge-link formulation (EGF), and to the Hamiltonian in Eq. ( 9 ) and its corresponding Hilbert space spanned by states in Eq. ( 13 ) as the minimal gauge-link formulation (MGF) of a Z 2 subscript 𝑍 2 Z_{2} italic_Z start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT LGT in (1+1)D with PBCs. 2 2 2 Such alternative formulations have been studied in the past for different LGTs with PBCs, e.g., in Ref.  Nagele  et al. ( 2019 ); Zache ( 2020 ); Zache  et al. ( 2019 ) . 
Quantum simulation of MGF requires fewer qubits than the EGF, as can be seen from mapping of fermionic and bosonic degrees of freedom to qubits.
The fermions in this work are mapped to qubits via the Jordan-Wigner transformation

Hence, in both formulation, N 𝑁 N italic_N qubits are needed to represent the fermionic Hilbert space.
The hardcore bosons are mapped to qubits directly with the s 𝑠 s italic_s state denoting the corresponding qubit eigenstate in the z-basis.
The qubit-resource savings is evident: to express the bosonic Hilbert space, the MGF needs only one qubit while the EGF requires N 𝑁 N italic_N qubits.
In this work, we use the MGF for simulating scattering in a Z 2 subscript 𝑍 2 Z_{2} italic_Z start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT LGT, which requires N + 1 𝑁 1 N+1 italic_N + 1 qubits for mapping the Hilbert space of N 𝑁 N italic_N ( N P subscript 𝑁 𝑃 N_{P} italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ) staggered (physical) lattice sites.

 In the next subsection, we discuss how to build the single-particle wave-packet state that comprises the initial scattering state. 

 II.2 Ansatz for meson creation operators in the interacting theory

 To simulate two-meson scattering in a (1+1)D Z 2 subscript 𝑍 2 Z_{2} italic_Z start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT LGT on a quantum computer, one first needs to prepare an initial state made up of two single-particle wave packets, ideally separated far away from each other.
The form of the single-particle wave packets | Ψ ⟩ ket Ψ \ket{\Psi} | start_ARG roman_Ψ end_ARG ⟩ follows our earlier work in Ref.  Davoudi  et al. ( 2024a ) . This optimization can be done using a variational quantum eigensolver (VQE). The ansatz in Ref.  Davoudi  et al. ( 2024a ) was motivated by a similar ansatz proposed earlier in Ref.  Rigobello  et al. ( 2021 ) in the context of tensor-network calculations of scattering in a U(1) gauge theory. 

 In this work, we propose a closely-related alternative ansatz whose form is motivated by the translation symmetry of the physical lattice. It uses kinematic factors similar to the ansatz in Refs.  Rigobello  et al. ( 2021 ); Davoudi  et al. ( 2024a ) , which are obtained by solving the free fermionic theory on a staggered lattice.
The translation symmetry of the staggered lattice, where a unit translation on the original (physical) lattice turns into a translation by two units on the staggered sites, is incorporated in the ansatz by considering the gauge-invariant operators ℳ m , n subscript ℳ 𝑚 𝑛 \mathcal{M}_{m,n} caligraphic_M start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_m , italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT .
The operator ℳ m , n subscript ℳ 𝑚 𝑛 \mathcal{M}_{m,n} caligraphic_M start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_m , italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT for the simple case of m = n 𝑚 𝑛 m=n italic_m = italic_n reduces to the fermion number operator ξ m † ⁢ ξ m subscript superscript 𝜉 † 𝑚 subscript 𝜉 𝑚 \xi^{\dagger}_{m}\xi_{m} italic_ξ start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT † end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_m end_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_ξ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_m end_POSTSUBSCRIPT .
However, when m ≠ n 𝑚 𝑛 m\neq n italic_m ≠ italic_n , ℳ m , n subscript ℳ 𝑚 𝑛 \mathcal{M}_{m,n} caligraphic_M start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_m , italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT is given by a non-local operator called a bare-meson creation operator.
In the EGF, this operator is composed of ξ m † subscript superscript 𝜉 † 𝑚 \xi^{\dagger}_{m} italic_ξ start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT † end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_m end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , ξ n subscript 𝜉 𝑛 \xi_{n} italic_ξ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , and a string of σ ~ l 𝐱 subscript superscript ~ 𝜎 𝐱 𝑙 \tilde{\sigma}^{\mathbf{x}}_{l} over~ start_ARG italic_σ end_ARG start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT bold_x end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_l end_POSTSUBSCRIPT operators connecting them.
For m < n 𝑚 𝑛 m<n italic_m < italic_n and the periodic lattice, there can be two ways of constructing such an operator: ξ m † ⁢ ( ∏ l = m n − 1 σ ~ l 𝐱 ) ⁢ ξ n subscript superscript 𝜉 † 𝑚 superscript subscript product 𝑙 𝑚 𝑛 1 subscript superscript ~ 𝜎 𝐱 𝑙 subscript 𝜉 𝑛 \xi^{\dagger}_{m}\,(\prod_{l=m}^{n-1}\tilde{\sigma}^{\mathbf{x}}_{l})\,\xi_{n} italic_ξ start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT † end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_m end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ( ∏ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_l = italic_m end_POSTSUBSCRIPT start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_n - 1 end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT over~ start_ARG italic_σ end_ARG start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT bold_x end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_l end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ) italic_ξ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT or ξ n ⁢ ( ∏ l = n N − 1 σ ~ l 𝐱 ) ⁢ ( ∏ l = 0 m − 1 σ ~ l 𝐱 ) ⁢ ξ m † subscript 𝜉 𝑛 superscript subscript product 𝑙 𝑛 𝑁 1 subscript superscript ~ 𝜎 𝐱 𝑙 superscript subscript product 𝑙 0 𝑚 1 subscript superscript ~ 𝜎 𝐱 𝑙 subscript superscript 𝜉 † 𝑚 \xi_{n}\,(\prod_{l=n}^{N-1}\tilde{\sigma}^{\mathbf{x}}_{l})(\prod_{l=0}^{m-1}%

 In this confined theory, it is expected that the operators which have a support over an extent much greater than the confinement scale will be suppressed exponentially.

 II.3 States’ fidelities with the ansatz meson creation operators

 [MATH:Refer to caption]

Each column corresponds to a non-negative momentum k 𝑘 k italic_k in the Brillouin zone, and each row corresponds to the order of the ansatz j 𝑗 j italic_j . Higher fidelity is observed for | k op ( j ) ⟩ ket subscript superscript 𝑘 𝑗 op \ket{k^{(j)}_{\rm op}} | start_ARG italic_k start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( italic_j ) end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_op end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_ARG ⟩ when larger values of m f , | ϵ | subscript 𝑚 𝑓 italic-ϵ m_{f},|\epsilon| italic_m start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_f end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , | italic_ϵ | , and a higher ansatz order j 𝑗 j italic_j are used. For example, F = 0.99 𝐹 0.99 F=0.99 italic_F = 0.99 is achieved at j = 3 𝑗 3 j=3 italic_j = 3 in the entire parameter space for all k 𝑘 k italic_k . The region to the left of the cyan contour corresponds to the existence of a low-energy non-mesonic excitation that is not captured by our mesonic ansatz. 

 In this section, we demonstrate the accuracy with which the optimized ansatz represents the desired states at a given order. In small systems, the accuracy can be checked by calculating the fidelity between the momentum eigenstates obtained by exact diagonalization and the optimized ansatz.
We define the fidelity of a hadron state in the momentum sector k 𝑘 k italic_k as 

 F \coloneq | ⟨ k ( j ) | k ⟩ ex op | 2 . F\coloneq{\left|\,{}_{\rm op}\langle k^{(j)}|k\rangle_{\rm ex}\right|}^{2}. italic_F | start_FLOATSUBSCRIPT roman_op end_FLOATSUBSCRIPT ⟨ italic_k start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( italic_j ) end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT | italic_k ⟩ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_ex end_POSTSUBSCRIPT | start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT .

 Here, | k ( j ) ⟩ op subscript ket superscript 𝑘 𝑗 op \ket{k^{(j)}}_{\rm op} | start_ARG italic_k start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( italic_j ) end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT end_ARG ⟩ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_op end_POSTSUBSCRIPT is prepared by acting on | Ω ⟩ ex subscript ket Ω ex \ket{\Omega}_{\rm ex} | start_ARG roman_Ω end_ARG ⟩ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_ex end_POSTSUBSCRIPT with the optimized ansatz for b k ( j ) ⁣ † subscript superscript 𝑏 𝑗 † 𝑘 b^{(j)\dagger}_{k} italic_b start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( italic_j ) † end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_k end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , where | Ω ⟩ ex subscript ket Ω ex \ket{\Omega}_{\rm ex} | start_ARG roman_Ω end_ARG ⟩ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_ex end_POSTSUBSCRIPT is the ground state obtained by numerically diagonalizing the Hamiltonian.
Similarly, | k ⟩ ex subscript ket 𝑘 ex \ket{k}_{\rm ex} | start_ARG italic_k end_ARG ⟩ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_ex end_POSTSUBSCRIPT is the lowest-energy mesonic eigenstate with momentum k 𝑘 k italic_k obtained from the exactly diagonalized Hamiltonian. 

 Figure 1 shows the fidelity scan in a 10-(staggered) site theory over a range of Hamiltonian parameters, m f subscript 𝑚 𝑓 m_{f} italic_m start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_f end_POSTSUBSCRIPT and ϵ italic-ϵ \epsilon italic_ϵ , for different k 𝑘 k italic_k -momentum sectors, and at various ansatz orders.
The order-by-order optimization is conducted in the following manner: at the j th superscript 𝑗 th j^{\rm th} italic_j start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT roman_th end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT order, a total of 2 ⁢ j 2 𝑗 2j 2 italic_j parameters α 0 / 1 ( j ′ ) , k superscript subscript 𝛼 0 1 superscript 𝑗 ′ 𝑘 \alpha_{0/1}^{(j^{\prime}),k} italic_α start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 0 / 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( italic_j start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ′ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ) , italic_k end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT with j ′ ≤ j superscript 𝑗 ′ 𝑗 j^{\prime}\leq j italic_j start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ′ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ≤ italic_j are optimized. The initial values of the first 2 ⁢ j − 2 2 𝑗 2 2j-2 2 italic_j - 2 parameters α 0 / 1 ( j ′ ) , k superscript subscript 𝛼 0 1 superscript 𝑗 ′ 𝑘 \alpha_{0/1}^{(j^{\prime}),k} italic_α start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 0 / 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( italic_j start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ′ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ) , italic_k end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT with j ′ < j superscript 𝑗 ′ 𝑗 j^{\prime}<j italic_j start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ′ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT < italic_j have been set by the optimized results at the previous ( j − 1 ) th superscript 𝑗 1 th ({j-1})^{\rm th} ( italic_j - 1 ) start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT roman_th end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT order, and their variation are limited to a window w = 0.1 × max ⁢ ( | α 0 / 1 ( j ′ ) , k | , 1 ) 𝑤 0.1 max superscript subscript 𝛼 0 1 superscript 𝑗 ′ 𝑘 1 w=0.1\times{\rm max}(|\alpha_{0/1}^{(j^{\prime}),k}|,1) italic_w = 0.1 × roman_max ( | italic_α start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 0 / 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( italic_j start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ′ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ) , italic_k end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT | , 1 ) to aid the optimization at the j th superscript 𝑗 th j^{\rm th} italic_j start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT roman_th end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT order. 

 As mentioned earlier, the j th superscript 𝑗 th j^{\rm th} italic_j start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT roman_th end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT order ansatz consists of bare mesons with length up to j 𝑗 j italic_j in position space.
Therefore, it is reasonable to expect that a higher-order ansatz can better capture the interacting momentum eigenstate | k ⟩ ket 𝑘 \ket{k} | start_ARG italic_k end_ARG ⟩ .
Also, as the order j 𝑗 j italic_j is increased, the ansatz is expected to improve in capturing longer-range correlations.
These expectations are in agreement with our numerical results.
The fidelity can reach 0.98 (0.99) for all the parameter range considered if the first-(third-)order ansatz is used, and the optimization at all orders works better in the strong-coupling regime where the mass gap is larger.
We also note that the infidelities in | k ( j ) ⟩ op subscript ket superscript 𝑘 𝑗 op \ket{k^{(j)}}_{\rm op} | start_ARG italic_k start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( italic_j ) end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT end_ARG ⟩ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_op end_POSTSUBSCRIPT are associated with contamination from the excited states in the same momentum sector k 𝑘 k italic_k , since our ansatz transforms properly under translation by construction. 

 In the k = 0 𝑘 0 k=0 italic_k = 0 sector, there is one non-mesonic configuration that can be constructed by flipping the single bosonic link, which cannot be captured by the ansatz.
Such an excitation corresponds to one unit of electric flux across the periodic lattice in the EGF, and has an energy proportional to N 𝑁 N italic_N . It, thus, does not generally appear in the low-energy spectrum.
Nonetheless, for sufficiently small ϵ italic-ϵ \epsilon italic_ϵ at a given m f subscript 𝑚 𝑓 m_{f} italic_m start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_f end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , i.e., the area to the left of the cyan contour in Fig.  1 , such a non-mesonic excitation can have energies lower than the single-particle momentum states.
In this region, | k = 0 ⟩ ex subscript ket 𝑘 0 ex \ket{k=0}_{\rm ex} | start_ARG italic_k = 0 end_ARG ⟩ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_ex end_POSTSUBSCRIPT is defined as the first mesonic excitation in the k = 0 𝑘 0 k=0 italic_k = 0 sector, and the fidelity is calculated accordingly. 

 Compared to the results in our recent work  Davoudi  et al. ( 2024a ) , which adopts a momentum-space ansatz containing only two parameters overall, the order-by-order translationally invariant position-space ansatz developed here performs better, even at the edge of the Brillouin zone (i.e., for large momenta).
The ability to create accurate momentum eigenstates with large k 𝑘 k italic_k yields faster-moving wave packets.
This, first of all, injects more energy into the process and leads more interesting outgoing scattering channels. Second, it reduces the total evolution time needed to bring the wave packets together in a scattering simulation; therefore, fewer Trotter steps and shallower circuits are needed for a Trotterized time evolution. 

 Finally, we note that the optimized values of α 0 / 1 ( j ) , k superscript subscript 𝛼 0 1 𝑗 𝑘 \alpha_{0/1}^{(j),k} italic_α start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 0 / 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( italic_j ) , italic_k end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT are not unique with different initializations of the optimization routine. However, we observe that the normalization condition for 𝒩 𝒩 \mathcal{N} caligraphic_N renders the resulting C m , n ( j ) subscript superscript 𝐶 𝑗 𝑚 𝑛 C^{(j)}_{m,n} italic_C start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( italic_j ) end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_m , italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT coefficients out of these initializations approximately identical. (The C m , n ( j ) subscript superscript 𝐶 𝑗 𝑚 𝑛 C^{(j)}_{m,n} italic_C start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( italic_j ) end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_m , italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT coefficients determine the gate parameters in the quantum circuit.)
It is also seen that the optimized α 0 / 1 ( j ) , k superscript subscript 𝛼 0 1 𝑗 𝑘 \alpha_{0/1}^{(j),k} italic_α start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 0 / 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( italic_j ) , italic_k end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT can be different between k 𝑘 k italic_k and − k 𝑘 -k - italic_k momentum sectors, as opposed to being symmetric like the ansatz in Ref.  Davoudi  et al. ( 2024a ) . 4 4 4 We have tried a symmetric ansatz with respect to k → − k → 𝑘 𝑘 k\to-k italic_k → - italic_k , which is identical to ansatz in Eq. ( 21 ) except one needs to include a factor of i | m − n | superscript 𝑖 𝑚 𝑛 i^{|m-n|} italic_i start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT | italic_m - italic_n | end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT in both sums. We have checked that, despite restoring the symmetry, this ansatz overall returns lower fidelities than the anstaz based on Eq. ( 21 ). We, therefore, do not consider such an ansatz any further. 
Only the positive- k 𝑘 k italic_k fidelities are plotted in Fig.  1 for brevity. (Similar fidelities are obtained for the negative- k 𝑘 k italic_k states). 

 The order-by-order ansatz is expected to work for larger systems, as demonstrated in Appendix  A for a 26-(staggered) site theory.
For lattice sizes that exceed the regime of viability of exact diagonalization, we compare the ansatz against matrix-product states (MPS), which can accurately approximate the exact eigenstates in (1+1)D, upon employing a density-matrix-renormalization-group (DMRG) method.
A parameter scan similar to Fig.  1 , but in the 26-(staggered) site theory, is presented in Fig.  11 , demonstrating high fidelities achieved even with the lowest-order ansatzes. 

 For the remainder of the main text, to reduce the clutter, we drop the superscript ( j ) 𝑗 (j) ( italic_j ) from quantities, since the order at which they are assumed will be clear from the context. 

 III Quantum algorithm and circuit design

 [MATH:Refer to caption] 
 Figure 2: Shown is the circuit used in this work for simulating scattering in a (1+1)D Z 2 subscript 𝑍 2 Z_{2} italic_Z start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT LGT in the MGF. The circuit acts on N + 1 𝑁 1 N+1 italic_N + 1 system qubits representing the lattice associated with N 𝑁 N italic_N staggered sites, and one or more ancilla qubits denoted by a 1 , 2 , t subscript 𝑎 1 2 𝑡 a_{1,2,t} italic_a start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 , 2 , italic_t end_POSTSUBSCRIPT . The system qubits are initialized in the SCV state | Ω ⟩ SCV subscript ket Ω SCV \ket{\Omega}_{\text{SCV}} | start_ARG roman_Ω end_ARG ⟩ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT SCV end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , as shown by the first vertical dotted line, while each ancilla starts in the | 0 ⟩ ket 0 \ket{0} | start_ARG 0 end_ARG ⟩ state. The circuit consists of three subcircuit modules. The vertical dotted lines indicate the state of the system qubits after the application of each module. The first module prepares the ground state, | Ω ⟩ ket Ω \ket{\Omega} | start_ARG roman_Ω end_ARG ⟩ , of the Hamiltonian in Eq. ( 16 ) using the circuit block 𝒬 GS subscript 𝒬 GS \mathcal{Q}_{\rm GS} caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_GS end_POSTSUBSCRIPT with parameters θ h ⁣ ∗ superscript 𝜃 ℎ \theta^{h*} italic_θ start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_h ∗ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT and θ m ⁣ ∗ superscript 𝜃 𝑚 \theta^{m*} italic_θ start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_m ∗ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT . It requires at least one ancilla qubit to prepare the initial state, shown here with a black solid line. Alternatively, it can also be applied using an extra ancilla qubits, shown with a dotted black line, to improve the accuracy of preparing the target state, as explained in the text. Finally, the last module, 𝒬 Trott subscript 𝒬 Trott \mathcal{Q}_{\rm Trott} caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_Trott end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , performs the unitary time evolution U ⁢ ( t ) = e − i ⁢ t ⁢ H 𝑈 𝑡 superscript 𝑒 𝑖 𝑡 𝐻 U(t)=e^{-itH} italic_U ( italic_t ) = italic_e start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT - italic_i italic_t italic_H end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT under the Hamiltonian H 𝐻 H italic_H in Eq. ( 16 ). The circuit components in red are to perform a Hadamard test to compute the return probability of the initial state as a function of time; they can be omitted when measuring only the expectation values of diagonal operators. The Hadamard test requires an additional ancilla and a controlled application of 𝒬 Trott subscript 𝒬 Trott \mathcal{Q}_{\rm Trott} caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_Trott end_POSTSUBSCRIPT with control on the ancilla. Finally, the parameters θ h ⁣ ∗ superscript 𝜃 ℎ \theta^{h*} italic_θ start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_h ∗ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT and θ m ⁣ ∗ superscript 𝜃 𝑚 \theta^{m*} italic_θ start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_m ∗ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT are obtained using a VQE method that minimize the energy of the state prepared by the circuit with respect to the Hamiltonian in Eq. ( 16 ). 

 In this section, we use the Hamiltonian and the ansatz for hadron states defined in Sec.  II to lay out a digital quantum algorithm for performing multi-hadron scattering.
We choose to work with the MGF for a more efficient circuit implementation. 

 The overall protocol used in this work starts from the SCV state | Ω ⟩ SCV subscript ket Ω SCV \ket{\Omega}_{\text{SCV}} | start_ARG roman_Ω end_ARG ⟩ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT SCV end_POSTSUBSCRIPT in Eq. ( 14 ), which is one of the computational basis states.
The protocol is composed of three circuit modules: 

 1. 

 𝒬 GS subscript 𝒬 GS \mathcal{Q}_{\rm GS} caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_GS end_POSTSUBSCRIPT prepares the interacting vacuum | Ω ⟩ ket Ω \ket{\Omega} | start_ARG roman_Ω end_ARG ⟩ from | Ω ⟩ SCV subscript ket Ω SCV \ket{\Omega}_{\text{SCV}} | start_ARG roman_Ω end_ARG ⟩ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT SCV end_POSTSUBSCRIPT . 

 2. 

 𝒬 Init subscript 𝒬 Init \mathcal{Q}_{\rm Init} caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_Init end_POSTSUBSCRIPT constructs the initial scattering state comprised of well-separated wave packets. 

 3. 

 𝒬 Trott subscript 𝒬 Trott \mathcal{Q}_{\rm Trott} caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_Trott end_POSTSUBSCRIPT performs the Trotterized time evolution under the Hamiltonian in Eq. ( 16 ). 

 These three modules are depicted in Fig.  2 , with each module separated by a dotted line, indicating the corresponding quantum state prepared at the end of each stage.
Each circuit module will be discussed in the following. Computing observables may require its own circuit module, which we will partly discuss in Sec.  IV.4 and Appendix  D . 

 Before proceeding, we emphasize that our procedure constitutes a hybrid classical-quantum algorithm, where both the ground state in module 1 and the k 𝑘 k italic_k -momentum eigenstates required in module 2 are prepared using the VQE method.
In this work, the VQE results for the ground state and k 𝑘 k italic_k -momentum eigenstates are verified only through classical evaluation, in order to save quantum-computing resources.
However, in theory, it is possible to implement both VQE calculations on a quantum computer provided that the expectation value of the Hamiltonian operator can be determined with sufficient accuracy from the hardware results (which generally require a large number of measurements). 

 III.1 𝒬 GS subscript 𝒬 GS \mathcal{Q}_{\rm GS} caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_GS end_POSTSUBSCRIPT : Preparing the interacting vacuum

 III.2 𝒬 Init subscript 𝒬 Init \mathcal{Q}_{\rm Init} caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_Init end_POSTSUBSCRIPT : Preparing the initial scattering state

 The second module, 𝒬 Init ⁢ ( Ψ 1 , Ψ 2 ) subscript 𝒬 Init subscript Ψ 1 subscript Ψ 2 \mathcal{Q}_{\rm Init}(\Psi_{1},\Psi_{2}) caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_Init end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ( roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ) , prepares the initial scattering state of two wave packets, | Ψ 1 , Ψ 2 ⟩ ket subscript Ψ 1 subscript Ψ 2 \ket{\Psi_{1},\Psi_{2}} | start_ARG roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_ARG ⟩ .
Each wave packet is defined in Eq. ( 18 ) in terms of non-unitary operators b k † subscript superscript 𝑏 † 𝑘 b^{\dagger}_{k} italic_b start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT † end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_k end_POSTSUBSCRIPT s.
The wave packets thus need to be created by implementing the b k † subscript superscript 𝑏 † 𝑘 b^{\dagger}_{k} italic_b start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT † end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_k end_POSTSUBSCRIPT operators in an extended Hilbert space using one or two ancilla qubits, as shown in Fig.  4 (a) and (b), respectively.
Here, 𝒬 WP ⁢ ( Ψ i ) subscript 𝒬 WP subscript Ψ 𝑖 \mathcal{Q_{\rm WP}}(\Psi_{i}) caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_WP end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ( roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ) creates a single wave packet state | Ψ i ⟩ ket subscript Ψ 𝑖 \ket{\Psi_{i}} | start_ARG roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_ARG ⟩ using an ancilla qubit that is initiated in | 0 ⟩ ket 0 \ket{0} | start_ARG 0 end_ARG ⟩ .
The method in Fig.  4 (a) uses only one ancilla qubit for both 𝒬 WP ⁢ ( Ψ 1 ) subscript 𝒬 WP subscript Ψ 1 \mathcal{Q_{\rm WP}}(\Psi_{1}) caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_WP end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ( roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ) and 𝒬 WP ⁢ ( Ψ 2 ) subscript 𝒬 WP subscript Ψ 2 \mathcal{Q_{\rm WP}}(\Psi_{2}) caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_WP end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ( roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ) , with a σ x superscript 𝜎 x \sigma^{\textbf{x}} italic_σ start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT x end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT operator acting on the ancilla in between the two circuits. In Fig.  4 (b), on the other hand, each 𝒬 WP subscript 𝒬 WP \mathcal{Q_{\rm WP}} caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_WP end_POSTSUBSCRIPT uses a different ancilla qubit.
Compared to the latter, the former method uses fewer quantum resources but at a cost of lower accuracy of achieving the target state, as will be discussed below. 

 The circuit for 𝒬 WP subscript 𝒬 WP \mathcal{Q_{\rm WP}} caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_WP end_POSTSUBSCRIPT is based
on that in Ref.  Jordan  et al. ( 2014 ) for the momentum creation operators b k † subscript superscript 𝑏 † 𝑘 b^{\dagger}_{k} italic_b start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT † end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_k end_POSTSUBSCRIPT in a non-interacting scalar field theory, in which case b k † subscript superscript 𝑏 † 𝑘 b^{\dagger}_{k} italic_b start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT † end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_k end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ’s exact expressions are known.

If the fermion occupation is 1 (0) at even (odd) fermion sites, the bosonic qubit is acted with a σ ~ 𝐱 superscript ~ 𝜎 𝐱 \tilde{\sigma}^{\bf x} over~ start_ARG italic_σ end_ARG start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT bold_x end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT such that the evolved phase is consistent with the Hamiltonian H ϵ superscript 𝐻 italic-ϵ H^{\epsilon} italic_H start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_ϵ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT in Eq. ( 17c ).

Each layer of second-order Trotterized time evolution requires 18 ⁢ N P + 8 18 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 8 18N_{P}+8 18 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT + 8 CNOT gates. 

 This concludes the discussion on all the circuit elements involved in preparing the initial scattering state and its time evolution.
In the next section, we present the quantum-emulator and hardware results arising from implementing these circuits to study a scattering process. 

 IV Quantum hardware and emulator results

 The circuit layout shown in Fig.  2 was employed to study scattering in a (1+1)D Z 2 subscript 𝑍 2 Z_{2} italic_Z start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT LGT with dynamical matter.
The viability of this circuit was demonstrated by executing it on the IonQ Forte quantum computer—a Ytterbium-ion-based quantum computer with 32 qubits, and high-fidelity single- and two-qubit gates  Chen  et al. ( 2024 ) .
Due to limited device-time availability, the VQE optimizations required for the parameters in the 𝒬 GS subscript 𝒬 GS \mathcal{Q}_{\rm GS} caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_GS end_POSTSUBSCRIPT circuit, and for the parameters that characterize the ansatz in Eq. ( 20 ), as well as the Hadamard test for computing the return probability, were performed using a noiseless quantum-circuit emulator.
The quantum hardware was instead used to implement the vacuum and wave-packet preparation circuits with known parameters, and to evolve the wave packets in a Trotterized manner. A few noise-mitigation strategies are also employed to improve the outcome of the simulations.
All these results are presented and discussed in this section. 

 We work with two different system sizes, N P = 5 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 5 N_{P}=5 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 5 and N P = 13 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 13 N_{P}=13 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 13 , corresponding to N = 10 𝑁 10 N=10 italic_N = 10 and N = 26 𝑁 26 N=26 italic_N = 26 , or equivalently 11 11 11 11 and 27 27 27 27 qubits, respectively.
The Hilbert space with the symmetry restriction Q = N P 𝑄 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 Q=N_{P} italic_Q = italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT has 504 states for N P = 5 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 5 N_{P}=5 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 5 , and 20 , 801 , 200 20 801 200 20,801,200 20 , 801 , 200 states for N P = 13 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 13 N_{P}=13 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 13 .
The former system size is within the reach of the exact-diagonalization technique on a standard computer,
while the low-energy states of the latter system size could be obtained using the DMRG method  White ( 1992 ) applied to an MPS ansatz for the states  Perez-Garcia  et al. ( 2007 ) , as discussed in Appendix  A .
We further set m f = 1.0 subscript 𝑚 𝑓 1.0 m_{f}=1.0 italic_m start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_f end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 1.0 and ϵ = − 0.3 italic-ϵ 0.3 \epsilon=-0.3 italic_ϵ = - 0.3 , which are the parameters used in our previous work  Davoudi  et al. ( 2024a ) . This parameter set ensures that the ratio of the contribution to the vacuum energy from the non-diagonal Hamiltonian, H h superscript 𝐻 ℎ H^{h} italic_H start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_h end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT , to that from the diagonal Hamiltonian, H m + H ϵ superscript 𝐻 𝑚 superscript 𝐻 italic-ϵ H^{m}+H^{\epsilon} italic_H start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_m end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT + italic_H start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_ϵ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT , is approximately 0.221 0.221 0.221 0.221 in the ground state for both N P = 5 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 5 N_{P}=5 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 5 and N P = 13 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 13 N_{P}=13 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 13 . This choice, therefore, puts us in a non-trivial regime of parameters, away from the strong- or weak-coupling limits. 

 IV.1 Variational-quantum-eigensolver optimization

 The VQE optimization for θ h ⁣ ∗ superscript 𝜃 ℎ \theta^{h*} italic_θ start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_h ∗ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT and θ m ⁣ ∗ superscript 𝜃 𝑚 \theta^{m*} italic_θ start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_m ∗ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT parameters can proceed via the quantum circuit introduced in Sec.  III.1 . The results for these parameters, as well as the fidelity of the prepared ground state, are summarized in Table  5 in Appendix  G .
These results clearly indicate that the ground state prepared after the first module in Fig.  2 is a very good approximation of the true ground state | Ω ⟩ ket Ω \ket{\Omega} | start_ARG roman_Ω end_ARG ⟩ . Next, the parameters that characterize the ansatz for the low-lying momentum-eigenstate creation operator b k † subscript superscript 𝑏 † 𝑘 b^{\dagger}_{k} italic_b start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT † end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_k end_POSTSUBSCRIPT in Eq. ( 20 ) are obtained for each target momentum k t ∈ Γ ~ subscript 𝑘 𝑡 ~ Γ k_{t}\in\widetilde{\Gamma} italic_k start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_t end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ∈ over~ start_ARG roman_Γ end_ARG .
One performs a VQE energy minimization for the states arising from 𝒬 WP ⁢ ( Ψ ) subscript 𝒬 WP Ψ \mathcal{Q}_{\rm WP}(\Psi) caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_WP end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ( roman_Ψ ) acted on | Ω ⟩ ket Ω \ket{\Omega} | start_ARG roman_Ω end_ARG ⟩ with Ψ ⁢ ( k ) = δ k , k t Ψ 𝑘 subscript 𝛿 𝑘 subscript 𝑘 𝑡 \Psi(k)=\delta_{k,k_{t}} roman_Ψ ( italic_k ) = italic_δ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_k , italic_k start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_t end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_POSTSUBSCRIPT in Eq. ( 18 ).
We use the second-order Trotter formula on the angle π / 2 𝜋 2 \pi/2 italic_π / 2 in Eq. ( 34 ) with ten and two Trotter steps for N P = 5 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 5 N_{P}=5 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 5 and N P = 13 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 13 N_{P}=13 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 13 , respectively.
These choices lead to a small Trotter error while keeping the VQE optimization time manageable on the noiseless emulator.
The results of the VQE optimization and the corresponding fidelities are summarized in Tables  6 and  7 in Appendix  G . These results indicate that the target states are captured with a very high fidelity. 

 IV.2 Preparing the initial hadronic wave packets

We use the second-order Trotter formula with n ~ T subscript ~ 𝑛 𝑇 \tilde{n}_{T} over~ start_ARG italic_n end_ARG start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_T end_POSTSUBSCRIPT Trotter steps for the angle π / 2 𝜋 2 \pi/2 italic_π / 2 in Eq. ( 34 ).
Furthermore, to reduce the circuit depth, we choose to implement only the angles θ 𝜃 \theta italic_θ such that θ > θ c 𝜃 subscript 𝜃 𝑐 \theta>\theta_{c} italic_θ > italic_θ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_c end_POSTSUBSCRIPT .
The choice of Trotter order, the number of Trotter steps, and θ > θ c 𝜃 subscript 𝜃 𝑐 \theta>\theta_{c} italic_θ > italic_θ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_c end_POSTSUBSCRIPT impact the accuracy of each wave-packet preparation step.
The implications of such approximations have been thoroughly studied for a single-wave-packet preparation in our previous work  Davoudi  et al. ( 2024a ) .
Moreover, the number of ancillary qubits used in 𝒬 Init ⁢ ( Ψ 1 , Ψ 2 ) subscript 𝒬 Init subscript Ψ 1 subscript Ψ 2 \mathcal{Q}_{\rm Init}(\Psi_{1},\Psi_{2}) caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_Init end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ( roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ) , and the two wave-packets overlap ( Ψ 2 | Ψ 1 ) conditional subscript Ψ 2 subscript Ψ 1 \left(\Psi_{2}|\Psi_{1}\right) ( roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT | roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ) , introduce further systematic errors in the two-wave-packet state preparation, as discussed in Sec.  III.2 . 

 The various levels of approximations introduce systematic errors in the state preparation. Nonetheless, they offer control over the quantum resources used, i.e., the number of qubits, single-qubit, and two-qubit gates. We consider two different approximations (Appxs) to enable the computations given the quantum resources available to us: 

 I.

The Appx I results are in better agreement with the ideal results. Appx II results, nonetheless, are not far off. Adopting Appx II, therefore, is not unreasonable especially since its required resources are significantly lower than Appx I.
These results are obtained by discarding measurements for which the ancilla qubit(s) do not have the correct value(s) | 1 a 1 , 1 a 2 ⟩ ket subscript 1 subscript 𝑎 1 subscript 1 subscript 𝑎 2 \ket{1_{a_{1}},1_{a_{2}}} | start_ARG 1 start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_a start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , 1 start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_a start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_ARG ⟩ for Appx I and | 1 a 1 ⟩ ket subscript 1 subscript 𝑎 1 \ket{1_{a_{1}}} | start_ARG 1 start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_a start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_ARG ⟩ for Appx II.

More states are discarded in Appx I than Appx II, since a part of the erroneous states are hidden in the valid ancilla values in Appx II, see discussions in Sec.  III.2 .

are obtained from bootstrap resampling of the global-symmetry-based noise-mitigated hardware results.
The ranges for the y-axes in all plots are taken to be over all possible values the corresponding observable can take. The axis for E 𝐸 E italic_E values is broken and rescaled appropriately to resolve the closely located data points near its maximum allowed value.

 The two-wave-packet preparation circuits within Appx II were executed on the IonQ Forte quantum computer, with 1000 shots per circuit. The χ n subscript 𝜒 𝑛 \chi_{n} italic_χ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT and E 𝐸 E italic_E values obtained from these runs are shown in Fig.  7 .
The hardware results contain various errors that are sourced from the trapped-ion quantum devices.
Similar to Ref.  Davoudi  et al. ( 2024a ) , we employ a simple post-processing error-mitigation scheme based on the global symmetry of the fermion-qubits configurations. Explicitly, we discard the states from the final results that exhibit Q ≠ N P 𝑄 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 Q\neq N_{P} italic_Q ≠ italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT .
Such states indicate the occurrence of at least one error in the fermion qubits.
We refer to this error as the symmetry-violation error, and denote it by Q̸ italic-Q̸ \not{Q} italic_Q̸ . We find Q̸ = 49.40 % italic-Q̸ percent 49.40 \not{Q}=49.40\,\% italic_Q̸ = 49.40 % for N P = 5 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 5 N_{P}=5 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 5 and Q̸ = 71.20 % italic-Q̸ percent 71.20 \not{Q}=71.20\,\% italic_Q̸ = 71.20 % for N P = 13 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 13 N_{P}=13 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 13 .
The states in the set complementary to Q̸ italic-Q̸ \not{Q} italic_Q̸ are not necessarily contamination free, since the errors that do not change Q 𝑄 Q italic_Q are not filtered away.
Part of these errors percolate into the ancilla-violation error: a̸ = 14.82 % italic-a̸ percent 14.82 \not{a}=14.82\,\% italic_a̸ = 14.82 % for N P = 5 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 5 N_{P}=5 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 5 and a̸ = 18.40 % italic-a̸ percent 18.40 \not{a}=18.40\,\% italic_a̸ = 18.40 % for N P = 13 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 13 N_{P}=13 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 13 . These are larger than their respective noiseless-simulator results in Table  3 , due to additional hardware errors. The error a̸ italic-a̸ \not{a} italic_a̸ is calculated over the shots remained after discarding those with a Q̸ italic-Q̸ \not{Q} italic_Q̸ error. States with the incorrect values of ancilla measurement are also discarded before evaluating the observables’ expectation-values.
Finally, the error bars in Fig.  7 are obtained from the standard deviation of the mean of the bootstrap samples of the physical events with 100 100 100 100 resampled configurations (at which value the bootstrap-sample mean distributions is stabilized). 

 The hardware results are in a good agreement with their noiseless-simulator counterparts for both observables.
The deviation from noiseless simulator is generally more prominent at the center of the wave packet. This feature can be attributed to the fact that the qubits at the center of the wave packets are acted on by gates with larger angles (i.e., larger | C m , n | subscript 𝐶 𝑚 𝑛 |C_{m,n}| | italic_C start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_m , italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT | values as seen in Fig.  6 ). Larger gate angles indicate longer gate-implementation times, making the gates more susceptible to quantum decoherence.
In summary, the ability to systematically control the various levels of approximation allows us to compromise marginally on the accuracy of preparing the initial scattering state while benefiting from significant reduction in quantum-resource requirements.
This is especially useful if the observable under investigation is local, and is, hence, robust against extensive error accumulation under time evolution  Trivedi  et al. ( 2024 ); Kashyap  et al. ( 2025 ) .
We further investigate this point in Secs.  IV.3 and  IV.4 by studying different observables under time evolution. 

 Finally, our method can be extended to prepare multiple spatially spaced wave packets.
One can repeat the application of 𝒬 WP ⁢ ( Ψ i ) subscript 𝒬 WP subscript Ψ 𝑖 \mathcal{Q}_{\rm WP}(\Psi_{i}) caligraphic_Q start_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_WP end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ( roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT ) with one or more ancilla qubits as many times as needed. We have demonstrated the preparation of three wave packets in an N P = 13 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 13 N_{P}=13 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 13 system on the IonQ Forte , see Appendix  C , observing good agreement with the expected results. 

 IV.3 Time-evolved observables

 [MATH:Refer to caption] 
 Figure 8: Shown are the expectation values of the staggered density χ n subscript 𝜒 𝑛 \chi_{n} italic_χ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_n end_POSTSUBSCRIPT across lattice-site index n 𝑛 n italic_n , in a Trotterized time-evolved scattering state of two meson wave packets for N P = 5 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 5 N_{P}=5 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 5 with 11 system qubits. Each column (row) shares the x-axis (y-axis) label. The plot legends and error bars are the same as in Fig.  7 . The exact results correspond to the evaluation of time-evolution unitary matrix U ⁢ ( t ) = e − i ⁢ t ⁢ H 𝑈 𝑡 superscript 𝑒 𝑖 𝑡 𝐻 U(t)=e^{-itH} italic_U ( italic_t ) = italic_e start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT - italic_i italic_t italic_H end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT upon exact exponentiation of the Hamiltonian matrix for a given time t 𝑡 t italic_t , acting on the corresponding initial state in Fig.  7 (a). The noiseless-simulator and hardware results correspond to 5 × 10 5 5 superscript 10 5 5\times 10^{5} 5 × 10 start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT 5 end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT and 3000 3000 3000 3000 shots, respectively.
The quantum circuits for the Trotter time evolution are taken with time steps of δ ⁢ t = 1 𝛿 𝑡 1 \delta t=1 italic_δ italic_t = 1 .
The meshed squares for the t = 7 𝑡 7 t=7 italic_t = 7 and t = 8 𝑡 8 t=8 italic_t = 8 plots denote the hardware-noise-dominated results.
The number of single-qubit and CNOT gates implemented for the combined state-preparation and time-evolution circuits for each t 𝑡 t italic_t is provided in Table  4 .

 [MATH:Refer to caption] 
 Figure 9: Shown are the expectation values of the electric field E 𝐸 E italic_E at the boson qubit, in a Trotterized time-evolved scattering state of two wave packets for N P = 5 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 5 N_{P}=5 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 5 with 11 system qubits.
The hardware results (cyan triangles) are dominated by noise.
Results obtained using the IonQ Forte noisy emulator are shown in Appendix  F to demonstrate a possible recovery of the signal using additional noise-mitigation tools, such as Pauli twirling and operator decoherence renormalization, at the cost of additional quantum-processing time.

 We use the circuit for Trotterized time evolution via a second-order Trotter product formula shown in Sec.  III.3 to evolve an initial two-wave-packet state on an IonQ Forte quantum computer.
The N P = 5 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 5 N_{P}=5 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 5 parameters in Table  2 are chosen for this purpose since each Trotter time step involves a shallower circuit than the larger-system counterpart: each Trotter-step circuit block constitutes 204 single-qubit gates and 60 CNOT gates. The three modules in Fig.  2 are implemented with Appx II for 3000 shots and δ ⁢ t = 1 𝛿 𝑡 1 \delta t=1 italic_δ italic_t = 1 . The number of shots and the size of the Trotter step required to match the theoretical prediction are estimated using the noisy emulator provided for this device by IonQ . 6 6 6 For future simulations involving large system sizes, a viable strategy is to repeat experiment for a range of Trotter-step sizes and shot numbers to reach convergence in measured observables. 

 The device results are analyzed using the methods described in the previous subsection, and the Q̸ italic-Q̸ \not{Q} italic_Q̸ and a̸ italic-a̸ \not{a} italic_a̸ values calculated for each Trotter step are shown in Table  4 .

 Table 4: Shown are the values of the single- and two-qubit gate counts, symmetry-violation error Q̸ italic-Q̸ \not{Q} italic_Q̸ , and the ancilla-violation error a̸ italic-a̸ \not{a} italic_a̸ , for different Trotter steps t 𝑡 t italic_t in time evolution of two meson wave packets, resulted from the IonQ Forte device. The gate counts are associated with the transpiled quantum circuit by the Qiskit transpiler. (The raw gate counts can be obtained from those provided in Table  3 for the state preparation and the cost of each Trotter step of evolution given in the text.) 

 The growing Q̸ italic-Q̸ \not{Q} italic_Q̸ and a̸ italic-a̸ \not{a} italic_a̸ errors with each Trotter step indicate increasing noise in the device results with deeper circuits.
As mentioned before, the a̸ italic-a̸ \not{a} italic_a̸ error in the noiseless simulation denotes the systematic error due to the approximate nature of b k † subscript superscript 𝑏 † 𝑘 b^{\dagger}_{k} italic_b start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT † end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_k end_POSTSUBSCRIPT operators.
In this case, this error does not change with time evolution as the ancilla qubit used for the initial-state preparation does not participate in the time-evolution circuit.
Thus, the increasing a̸ italic-a̸ \not{a} italic_a̸ error with each Trotter step in Table  4 purely reflects the increasing hardware error. 

 The results for the time-evolved staggered density are shown in the Fig.  8 .
The values obtained from the quantum circuits are compared against the exact calculations that do not exhibit the Trotter error (i.e., they are obtained by calculating U ⁢ ( t ) 𝑈 𝑡 U(t) italic_U ( italic_t ) using exact matrix exponentiation).
From these results, the wave packets can be seen to be moving towards each other with their peak values decreasing with time.
The noiseless results (using 5 × 10 5 5 superscript 10 5 5\times 10^{5} 5 × 10 start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT 5 end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT shots) in both approximations (Appxs I and II) follow the exact wave-packet profile in the early times, but deviate from the exact values with increasing evolution time due to accumulated Trotter error. In fact, in some instances, the cruder, hence less resource-intensive, Appx II displays less Trotter error than Appx II. This observation makes it clear that small deviations in the initial states may be insignificant in subsequent simulation steps given the effect of other errors during the evolution.
Nonetheless, the shape of the profile for both initial-state approximations resembles the exact wave-packet evolution, and still offers qualitative description of the physical process. 

 The hardware results also show the qualitative features of time-evolved staggered density, however, the device noise starts to dominate after Trotter time t = 6 𝑡 6 t=6 italic_t = 6 .
Furthermore, our results are obtained without performing any additional error-mitigation-circuit runs and only by discarding the a̸ italic-a̸ \not{a} italic_a̸ and Q̸ italic-Q̸ \not{Q} italic_Q̸ errors during post-processing.
Nonetheless, we have checked that discarding the a̸ italic-a̸ \not{a} italic_a̸ and Q̸ italic-Q̸ \not{Q} italic_Q̸ data does not significantly improve the outcome, and only leads to reduced statistics, hence larger shot noise. The reason can be attributed to the fact that most of symmetry-violated errors are associated with a few (mostly one) bit-flip errors in primarily random locations in the qubit register, whose effect becomes insignificant when computing expectation value of local operators. Our observation is consistent with the conclusions of Ref.  Nguyen  et al. ( 2022 ) , which finds that the effect of symmetry-based noise mitigation is both quantity dependent and time dependent. 

 We further compute the time evolution of the electric-field expectation value at the boson qubit, E 𝐸 E italic_E , as shown in Fig.  9 .
Here, the noiseless result agrees with the exact result up to Trotter errors, and almost retains its value at t = 0 𝑡 0 t=0 italic_t = 0 throughout the evolution. The reason is that flipping the electric field at the boson qubit costs an energy proportional to the system size, see Eq. ( 17c ). The hardware result for this quantity, on the other hand, significantly deviates from the exact value.
Thus, obtaining the Trotter time evolution of E 𝐸 E italic_E requires further noise-mitigation techniques.
We have demonstrated this in Appendix  F using the IonQ Forte noisy emulator where Pauli twirling  Wallman and Emerson ( 2016 ) , along with operator decoherence renormalization  Urbanek  et al. ( 2021 ); A Rahman  et al. ( 2022 ); Farrell  et al. ( 2024a ) , are employed to recover the time-evolved value of E 𝐸 E italic_E . 7 7 7 We relied on the noisy emulator for this analysis because such mitigation techniques require more quantum processing, and our access to the device was limited. 

 The hardware runs’ moderate coherence time, and time evolution’s large circuit depth, prohibit accessing interesting long-time scattering dynamics in this hardware study. Another issue is the small system size, leading to boundary affects in the simulation outcome in the long-time limit. One would, therefore, need to simulate evolution of wave packets in larger systems, but the associated circuit depths would increase considerably. Nonetheless, the results presented here marks the first hadron-scattering simulation on a quantum computer; 8 8 8 See also the parallel submission by Schuhmacher et al. in the same arXiv listing. it has pushed the limits of what is possible for such an involved simulation problem on any quantum hardware to date. 

 IV.4 Return probability

 [MATH:Refer to caption] 
 Figure 10: Shown is the return probability, ℛ ⁢ ( t ) ℛ 𝑡 \mathcal{R}(t) caligraphic_R ( italic_t ) , of the initial two wave-packet state for N P = 13 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 13 N_{P}=13 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 13 (27 system qubits) against time. The Trotter time step is δ ⁢ t = 0.25 𝛿 𝑡 0.25 \delta t=0.25 italic_δ italic_t = 0.25 , but results for only integer times are plotted. The MPS results are obtained using the TDVP algorithm, and the values for Appxs I and II cases are calculated using the circuit for the Hadamard test.

 Computing the scattering S 𝑆 S italic_S -matrix, which relates scattering states in early and late times, is a critical observable in nuclear and high-energy physics.
In this section, we compute the return probability (also known as the survival probability and Loschmidt echo): 

 ℛ ⁢ ( t ) ⁢ \coloneq ⁢ | ⟨ Ψ 1 , Ψ 2 | U ⁢ ( t ) | Ψ 1 , Ψ 2 ⟩ | 2 , ℛ 𝑡 \coloneq superscript quantum-operator-product subscript Ψ 1 subscript Ψ 2 𝑈 𝑡 subscript Ψ 1 subscript Ψ 2 2 \mathcal{R}(t)\coloneq\left|\braket{\Psi_{1},\Psi_{2}}{U(t)}{\Psi_{1},\Psi_{2}%
}\right|^{2}, caligraphic_R ( italic_t ) | ⟨ start_ARG roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_ARG | start_ARG italic_U ( italic_t ) end_ARG | start_ARG roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_ARG ⟩ | start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT , 

 (41) 

 which is a diagonal entry of the scattering S 𝑆 S italic_S -matrix.
Here, we restrict our discussion to the computation of return probability for the initial state consisting of the two wave packets prepared in Sec.  IV.2 , rather than its phenomenological implications, or more interesting, but significantly more involved, final-state-momentum-dependent overlaps. 

 Consider the case of N P = 13 subscript 𝑁 𝑃 13 N_{P}=13 italic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_P end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = 13 with the initial state | Ψ 1 , Ψ 2 ⟩ ket subscript Ψ 1 subscript Ψ 2 \ket{\Psi_{1},\Psi_{2}} | start_ARG roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT , roman_Ψ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_ARG ⟩ shown in Fig.  7 (b), using both approximations, Appxs I and II.
Recall that the former (latter) approximation requires more (less) quantum resources, and prepares a state with higher (lower) fidelity with respect to the target state.
The return probability for each approximation is computed using the Hadamard test, shown schematically in Fig.  2 and described in more detail in Appendix  D , using the noiseless Aer emulator.
The Trotter step size is taken to be δ ⁢ t = 0.25 𝛿 𝑡 0.25 \delta t=0.25 italic_δ italic_t = 0.25 to avoid a large Trotter error, and the results are shown in Fig.  10 only for integer times to avoid clutter.
The emulator results are compared against the ideal results obtained from optimizing an MPS ansatz for the state and performing the time evolution using the time-dependent variational-principle (TDVP) algorithm  Haegeman  et al. ( 2011 , 2016 ); Yang and White ( 2020 ) . 

 Figure  10 indicates that the return probability associated with Appx II deviates significantly from the ideal case, but that associated with Appx I agrees with the ideal result up to Trotter errors.
This can attributed to the non-local nature of the observable ℛ ⁢ ( t ) ℛ 𝑡 \mathcal{R}(t) caligraphic_R ( italic_t ) which is sensitive to the state vector.
Large deviations from the target state vector, like in Appx II, cause interference effects from the contamination when computing the probability in Eq. ( 41 ).
This is in contrast to local observables that are rather insensitive to small deviations from the target state, see Appendix  E for the example of local staggered density for the same simulation. 

 Preparing a high-fidelity initial state is, therefore, of crucial importance in future precision-physics applications of quantum computers, especially for observables like the S 𝑆 S italic_S -matrix elements that are highly sensitive to the state vector.
On the other hand, understanding qualitative behavior of local observables under time evolution is a more tractable problem for the NISQ devices, as demonstrated in Sec.  IV.3 . 

 V Conclusion and outlook

 We developed and implemented a digital algorithm for simulating scattering of hadrons on a quantum computer.
Focusing on a (1+1)D Z 2 subscript 𝑍 2 Z_{2} italic_Z start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT lattice gauge theory with fermionic matter, we first prepared the initial scattering state of well-separated single-hadron wave packets with well-defined localized momenta.
Our hybrid classical-quantum state-preparation algorithm offers control over various levels of approximations for preparing the initial state, such that the fidelity with the target state can be systematically improved at the cost of increasing quantum resources.
We prepared the two- and three-wave-packet state on the IonQ Forte trapped-ion-based quantum computer for systems with 10 10 10 10 and 26 26 26 26 fermionic sites, using one of these approximations, requiring significantly lower circuit depths, see Table  3 .
Even in the presence of hardware noise, results agree well with the ideal results, see Figs.  7 and  14 , and a symmetry-based noise mitigation yielded only small improvement. 

 We then performed a quantum time evolution using the second-order Trotter product formula for a system of 10 10 10 10 fermionic sites, which allowed for hadrons to evolve till they collide, see Fig.  8 .
Unfortunately, the device noise washed out the signal for the local fermion-density observable before the hadronic wave packets could fully collide.
Nonetheless, the early-evolution time data are encouraging, despite a large number of entangling gates used and no major noise-mitigation strategy implemented.
The electric-field energy stored in the remnant gauge link (after gauge fixing) exhibited significant decoherence even at the early steps of time evolution, see Fig.  9 .
Nonetheless, using a noisy emulator, we demonstrated the effectiveness of two noise-mitigation schemes, Pauli twirling together with operator decoherence renormalization, in recovering the electric-field value. 

 Finally, we emphasized the importance of preparing high-fidelity initial scattering states for future precision computations of state-sensitive observables, such as phase shifts, S 𝑆 S italic_S -matrix, and decay width.
Our computation of the return probability, which is a diagonal entry of the S 𝑆 S italic_S -matrix, demonstrates that approximations that may appear benign when measuring local observables, appear to exacerbate the error in overlap quantities such as return probability, see Fig.  10 . 

 In the following, we present an outlook of this study, including potential avenues for future research: 

 ⋄ ⋄ \diamond ⋄ 

 Our ansatz is both effective and efficient in creating single-particle hadronic states with high fidelity in (1+1)D Abelian theories (see our previous work for a U(1) example  Davoudi  et al. ( 2024a ) ).
Given the ultimate goal of preparing hadronic states in QCD on a quantum computer, the natural next step is to extend the ansatz to non-Abelian LGTs, and to LGTs in more than one spatial dimensions. These involve introducing additional gauge-invariant bare-meson operators, both arising from non-Abelian components of the fields, and from Wilson-loop operators in (2+1) and higher dimensions. One hopes that the ansatz can approximate the target hadronic state with high fidelity using only a polynomial number of parameters, even toward the continuum limit. 

 ⋄ ⋄ \diamond ⋄ 

 Quantum simulation of non-Abelian LGT dynamics in more than one spatial dimensions is a resource-intensive task  Byrnes and Yamamoto ( 2006 ); Shaw  et al. ( 2020 ); Ciavarella  et al. ( 2021 ); Kan and Nam ( 2021 ); Lamm  et al. ( 2019 ); Haase  et al. ( 2021 ); Davoudi  et al. ( 2023 ); Murairi  et al. ( 2022 ); Rhodes  et al. ( 2024 ); Lamm  et al. ( 2024 ); Balaji  et al. ( 2025 ) .
It is perceivable that various alternative formulations of the Kogut-Susskind Hamiltonian formulation of LGTs  Bañuls  et al. ( 2017 ); Raychowdhury and Stryker ( 2020 ); Kadam  et al. ( 2023 , 2024 ); Pardo  et al. ( 2023 ); Davoudi  et al. ( 2021b ); Chandrasekharan and Wiese ( 1997 ); Wiese ( 2021 ); Zohar and Burrello ( 2015 ); Zohar and Cirac ( 2018 , 2019 ); Alexandru  et al. ( 2024 , 2019 ); Ji  et al. ( 2023 , 2020 ); Kavaki and Lewis ( 2024 ); Illa  et al. ( 2025 ); D’Andrea  et al. ( 2024 ); Zache  et al. ( 2023 ); Hartung  et al. ( 2022 ); Romiti and Urbach ( 2024 ); Fontana  et al. ( 2024 ); Bauer and Grabowska ( 2023 ); Grabowska  et al. ( 2024 ); Burbano and Bauer ( 2024 ); Ciavarella  et al. ( 2025 , 2021 ) will alleviate some of the resource requirements.
Among these, the loop-string-hadron (LSH) formulation  Raychowdhury and Stryker ( 2020 ); Kadam  et al. ( 2023 , 2024 ) , is particularly appealing since its degrees of freedom are generated by fully local and gauge-invariant operators in the electric basis, and only an algebraic Abelian constraint remains to be enforced on each link of the lattice. This formulation, therefore, may present a cost-efficient framework for extending our ansatz. Bare-meson operators can be simply mapped to gauge-invariant states of the LSH formulation, and the Abelian constraints can aid noise mitigation in quantum-circuit implementations. 

 ⋄ ⋄ \diamond ⋄ 

 The single-hadron momentum eigenstates prepared using the algorithm of this work, and extended to more complex gauge theories, are crucially needed in studying hadron structure using quantum computers. Ultimately, one aims to constrain, from real-time correlators of hadrons, various non-perturbative QCD matrix elements of relevance to collider physics  Bauer ( 2025 ) , such as various distribution functions  Lamm  et al. ( 2020 ); Echevarria  et al. ( 2021 ); Li  et al. ( 2022 ); Mueller  et al. ( 2020 ); Pérez-Salinas  et al. ( 2021 ); Qian  et al. ( 2021 ); Pedernales  et al. ( 2014 ); Gustin and Goldstein ( 2022 ) for large-hadron and electron-ion colliders. The expressive form of our ansatz, and its potential extensions, may prove valuable in gaining more insights into such structure quantities in the future. 

 ⋄ ⋄ \diamond ⋄ 

 Our method is applicable to preparation of any number of hadronic wave-packets (as long as systems size is large enough to contain well-separated wave packets).
This feature is particularly useful when one aims to compute exclusive scattering amplitudes. A second qubit register is used to prepare the desired multi-hadron final state. Then a swap test  Buhrman  et al. ( 2001 ) can be used to compute the overlap between the time-evolved initial state and the desired final state.
Upon insertion of spacetime-separated electroweak currents, one can further access quantities such as hadron tensor. Thus, the optical theorem can be invoked to obtain the inclusive scattering cross section for a given center-of-mass energy.
However, both of these analyses require larger lattice sizes and evolution times, hence wider and deeper quantum circuits. 

 ⋄ ⋄ \diamond ⋄ 

 The scattering protocol presented here allows one to access the outgoing scattering state at any moment past collision. Therefore, not only can one access asymptotic observables such as the S 𝑆 S italic_S -matrix, but also can measure other local and non-local observables in early moments after the collision and beyond. A novel quantity of interest, not readily accessible in collider experiments, is entanglement, in form of bipartite entanglement entropy or entanglement Hamiltonian. There is significant interest in quantum simulations of string breaking, hadronization, fragmentation, and thermalization in the aftermath of high-energy hadronic collisions  Cochran  et al. ( 2024 ); De  et al. ( 2024 ); Gonzalez-Cuadra  et al. ( 2024 ); Ciavarella ( 2024 ); Crippa  et al. ( 2024 ); Liu  et al. ( 2024 ); Alexandrou  et al. ( 2025 ); Luo  et al. ( 2025 ); Zhou  et al. ( 2022 ); Mueller  et al. ( 2024 ); Li  et al. ( 2024c ); Farrell  et al. ( 2025b ); Lee  et al. ( 2024 ); Florio  et al. ( 2024 ); Davoudi  et al. ( 2024b ) . Nonetheless, many of early experimental studies are limited to simple models and scenarios, and do not involve high-energy hadronic collisions. Future quantum-simulation studies, built upon efficient high-energy hadronic-state preparation schemes (such as that presented in this work), can begin to shed light on such important non-equilibrium phenomenology. 

 Note: A parallel submission by J. Schuhmacher, G. X. Su, J. J. Osborne, A. Gandon, J. C. Halimeh, and I. Tavernelli is to appear in the same arXiv listing as our paper, where authors perform a quantum-simulation experiment of hadron scattering in a quantum-link-model formulation of the U(1) LGT on an IBM quantum computer.
